  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_29bb873f-3eae-4aa7-839b-e788ab7294b5'
      });

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2055997298557245838"><i>How to Read a Book,</i> originally published in 1940, has become a rare phenomenon, a <i>living</i> classic. It is the best and most successful guide to reading comprehension for the general reader. And now it has been completely rewritten and updated. <p> You are told about the various levels of reading and how to achieve them – from elementary reading, through systematic skimming and </p></span>
  <span id="freeText2055997298557245838" style="display:none"><i>How to Read a Book,</i> originally published in 1940, has become a rare phenomenon, a <i>living</i> classic. It is the best and most successful guide to reading comprehension for the general reader. And now it has been completely rewritten and updated. <p> You are told about the various levels of reading and how to achieve them – from elementary reading, through systematic skimming and inspectional reading, to speed reading, you learn how to pigeonhole a book, X-ray it, extract the author's message, criticize. You are taught the different reading techniques for reading practical books, imaginative literature, plays, poetry, history, science and mathematics, philosophy and social science. </p><p> Finally, the authors offer a recommended reading list and supply reading tests whereby you can measure your own progress in reading skills, comprehension and speed.<br /><br />This a previously-published edition of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41878914-how-to-read-a-book" rel="nofollow noopener">ISBN 9780671212094</a></p></span>
  <a data-text-id="2055997298557245838" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/616528-is-reading-this-really-worth-it-i-ve">Is reading this really worth it? I&#39;ve started but it&#39;s so boring and don&#39;t bring much to the table. Will this book really change something on my reading life? It seems it&#39;s only stuff I know covered up by beautiful words and tiresome repetitions.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_616528'><a id="like_count_community_question_616528" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/616528?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">16 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F567610.How_to_Read_a_Book">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/616528-is-reading-this-really-worth-it-i-ve">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/42882951-jignesh-darji"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1487701297i/42882951._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/42882951-jignesh-darji">Jignesh Darji</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Yes, this book is worth it. Yes, this book will change a lot on your reading. <br/><br/>This book was written in a very different time where authors like to tal<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Yes, this book is worth it. Yes, this book will change a lot on your reading. <br /><br />This book was written in a very different time where authors like to talk too much and convey too little. Author has filled it with sound advice wrapped in a lot of criticism of the education system and approach to education and reading. To really learn from the book, you'll need to see past the rhetoric. <br /><br />How to go about reading this book if you find it too boring, and/or repetitive: <br />- Look at the outline - understand the structure of the book and draw the big picture. <br />- Read a summary of the levels of reading the author mentions (Elementary, Inspectional, Analytical, and Syntopical) and the steps to achieve each level. The book is about these 4 levels and how to achieve them. That's all. If you know this, you can skip all the rhetoric and finish the book really quickly while still taking-in all that the author wanted to convey. <br /><br />I hope this will be of some help. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/302437-my-problem-is-that-i-can-t-recall-what-i">My problem is that I can&#39;t recall what I read to be able to describe it to someone else. I have Bipolar Disorder &amp; not remembering things is a side effect of it. Will this book help me recall the main points of what I read enough to describe it to someone else? I love reading &amp; read non-fiction Christian books one right after the other.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_302437'><a id="like_count_community_question_302437" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/302437?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">4 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F567610.How_to_Read_a_Book">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/302437-my-problem-is-that-i-can-t-recall-what-i">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/302437-my-problem-is-that-i-can-t-recall-what-i">See all 5 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/14865479-claire"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1387066345i/14865479.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/14865479-claire">Claire</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">What you describe sounds more like a learning disability (LD): LD can be comorbid with other psychological/cognitive disorders or you can mimic such d<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">What you describe sounds more like a learning disability (LD): LD can be comorbid with other psychological/cognitive disorders or you can mimic such disorders when taking prescription drugs to treat other disorders.<br /><br />I am a former sufferer of depression, and a graduate in psychology. I struggled in school but, when I complained to my health care providers, they always blamed the disorder or the drugs and said there was nothing I could do. After suffering for more than a decade, I stopped treatment AMA (it worked well for me, but I do not recommend this) and wound up in remission (my body finally recovered but the drugs were interfering with my body asserting itself). I still struggled with reading problems. Finally, being off meds and medically diagnosed as in remission, they listened to me and referred me for testing: turns out, I have had Learning Disabilities - including a form of dyslexia - since birth and they were always blaming something else.<br /><br />My point is that Health Care Providers can get a little lazy, attributing everything to one diagnosis and missing the rest of the picture. I would ask your psychiatrist for help investigating this issue: some psychiatrists are qualified to diagnose cognitive issues/psychoeducational issues, most are not but they can refer you to an appropriate provider (usually a psychoeducational psychologist) for testing and consult. If you can afford it or your insurance will pay for it, it is worth it even later in life.<br /><br />This book probably WON'T help with your issue: this book discusses different methods of reading, and changing your method will change your processing of the material, which may alter your memory of the material, it does not help with working memory. It will teach you how to find the main points, but that may not be enough if you have a memory issue. <br /><br />That said, book club members often take notes as they go along and bring them to group: if you keep a book journal, know how to analyse such things and write them down as they occur to you (with an extra "download" or review when you finish the book), you will have more than enough material to describe and discuss. Going over notes will also strengthen your memory, and this book will teach you to take notes.<br /><br />Good luck!<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_216689895" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/216689895" />
    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/216689895?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6237864-nandakishore-varma?shelf=literary-craft">literary-craft</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer216689895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3324895668215847918">How do you read a book?<br/><br/>Look at the cover, probably glance at the blurb; run your eye down the table of contents, perhaps; possibly rifle through the book... then plunge right in into Chapter One.<br/><br/>Right?<br/><br/>Wrong! According to Mortimer J. Adler and Charles Van Doren, the authors of <i>How to Read a Book</i>.<br/><br/>According to them, this is only the first level of reading, called “Elementary” reading: and this is the only level the majority of readers in this world have reached. They posit three more levels: “Inspe</span>
  <span id="freeText3324895668215847918" style="display:none">How do you read a book?<br /><br />Look at the cover, probably glance at the blurb; run your eye down the table of contents, perhaps; possibly rifle through the book... then plunge right in into Chapter One.<br /><br />Right?<br /><br />Wrong! According to Mortimer J. Adler and Charles Van Doren, the authors of <i>How to Read a Book</i>.<br /><br />According to them, this is only the first level of reading, called “Elementary” reading: and this is the only level the majority of readers in this world have reached. They posit three more levels: “Inspectional”, “Analytical” and “Syntopic”, each one more advanced than the previous. The major portion of the book is devoted to analytic reading, followed by brief exposition on the syntopic. It is the aim of the authors to make each reader of this tome into an analytic reader at least, if not a syntopic one: it is my opinion that they only succeed partially, but let’s go into that after analysing each of the levels as defined by the authors.<br /><br />Elementary reading we have already seen. In inspectional reading, you first skim the book as a whole; give it a “once-over”, as it is. The authors, ever practical, suggest six steps to do this – most of them self-evident and what any serious reader usually does with an expository book (this book is mostly about reading expository material and of limited value in reading literature and poetry, but more about that later). The steps are:<br /><br />1.	Read the title and the preface<br />2.	Study the table of contents<br />3.	Check the index<br />4.	Read the blurb<br />5.	Look at the main chapters<br />6.	Skim the book, reading it here and there<br /><br />Next, read the book through fast, without getting stuck at the difficult places. If the book deserves our serious attention, we can come back to those difficult places in our next reading. The advantage of this “rapid-fire” approach is that we do not waste time on a book which deserves only a superficial reading. In the authors’ own words: “Every book should be read no more slowly than it deserves, and no more quickly than you can read it with satisfaction and comprehension.”<br /><br /><b>Analytical Reading</b><br /><br />The next level, analytical reading, requires the reader to be demanding: the more you demand, the more you can extract out of a book. To do this, one has to ask four questions:<br /><br />1.	What is the book about, as a whole?<br />2.	What is being said in detail, and how?<br />3.	Is the book true, in whole or part?<br />4.	What of it?<br /><br />How ask these four questions is explained in detail, in the remaining part of the book.<br /><br />Analytical reading has three stages. The first one is mainly concerned with classifying the book, and understanding its aim and structure. To do this, the authors suggest four rules.<br /><br />1.	<i>You must know what kind of book you are reading, and you should know as early in the process as possible, preferably before you begin to read.</i><br /><br />2.	<i>State the unity of the whole book in a single sentence, or at most a few sentences (a short paragraph).</i><br /><br />3.	<i>Set forth the major parts of the book, and show how these are organised into a whole, by being ordered to one another and to the unity of the whole.</i><br /><br />4.	<i>Find out what the author’s problems were.</i><br /><br />The first rule classifies (“pigeonholes”) the book, by affixing it to a category, genre, etc.: the second is used to create a précis: the third expands the précis into an outline, thus revealing the underlying structure (“X-Raying” the book, as the authors name it) and the fourth defines the purpose of the book. The author presumably wrote it for a reason: he had some questions at the beginning, which he has presumably tried to answer through the book. The reader has to find out what these questions are.<br /><br />If the first stage of analytical reading is related to the <i>
  <b>what</b>
</i>, the second is related to the <i>
  <b>how</b>
</i>; how has the author attempted to solve the problem with which he started out. For this stage also, Adler and Van Doren proposes four rules.<br /><br />1.	<i>Come to terms with the author by interpreting his key words.</i><br /><br />2.	<i>Grasp the author’s leading propositions by dealing with his most important sentences. </i><br /><br />3.	<i>Know the author’s arguments by finding them in, or constructing them out of, sequences of sentences.</i><br /><br />4.	<i>Determine which of his problems the author has solved, and which he has not: and as to the latter, decide which the author knew he had failed to solve.</i><br /><br />The argument here that any author, putting forth an argument, will use certain key words and terms (for example “natural selection” and “evolution” by Darwin in <i>The Origin of Species</i>). It is the reader’s duty to come to terms with the author, so that he does not misinterpret the author’s intentions by misreading the terms. Then on, it is an exercise in logic by understanding the propositions and arguments. This is not as difficult as it looks: in fact, we do it all the time, even though the exact logical terms may be unfamiliar to us. A proposition is nothing but the meaning contained within a declarative sentence: and arguments what the author uses to prove the truth of the proposition.<br /><br />The fourth step is a little more difficult for the lay reader, and it will only come through practice. One needs to find out which of the problems presented the author had been able to solve: and if he had been unable to solve some, whether he knew he had failed or not. At this point of time, it is not important whether the reader agrees with the author. That comes later. <i>Here, we are talking about the author’s own internal logic, and how far he has been able to present his arguments consistently in light of it, and how far he has been in successfully concluding his arguments.</i><br /><br />In the third stage of analytical reading, the reader, for the first time, starts to apply his critical senses and begins to agree or disagree with the author. Here according to the authors of the current book, the reader has to follow certain etiquette, captured in the following three rules:<br /><br />1.	<i>Do not begin criticism until one has completed the outline (first stage) and interpretation (second stage). Then one can agree, disagree or suspend judgement.</i><br /><br />2.	<i>Do not disagree disputatiously or contentiously. Or in plain words, unless one can present factual evidence acceptable at least to oneself, disagreement with an author based on emotional prejudice should be avoided (easier said than done!).</i><br /><br />3.	<i>Demonstrate that one knows the difference between knowledge and mere personal opinion by presenting good reasons for any critical judgement one makes.</i><br /><br />The authors also provide special criteria for criticism: (1) show where the author is uninformed, (2) show where he is misinformed, (3) show where his illogical and (4) show where his analysis is incomplete.<br /><br /><b>Syntopic Reading</b><br /><br />This is the fourth (and most advanced) level of reading, according to Adler and Van Doren – though I’d perhaps disagree. Here, the reader is engaged in researching books about one basic idea. For example, if you want to read up on, say evolution, you must first understand what the significant books are on the subject: then you must proceed to read them, and summarise the arguments, both pro and con, preferably remaining objective throughout. Phew! Not a very easy task.<br /><br />Don’t worry, the authors give step-by-step instructions for this level also. First, create a bibliography of the subject and inspect all of the books to ascertain which are the relevant ones: then, do the following:<br /><br />1.	Do inspectional reading of the selected book to choose the passages which are most relevant to the subject at hand;<br />	<br />2.	Establish a neutral terminology which is applicable to all the authors, so that all of them can be brought to the same terms;<br /><br />3.	Establish a set of neutral propositions, by framing a set of questions which all the authors can be seen as answering;<br /><br />4.	Range the answers on both sides of the issue. The issue may not always explicitly exist, and may have to be constructed by interpretation of the authors’ views (for example, in the case of evolutionary theory, “Intelligent Design” is a form of creationism even though the trappings of evolutionary theory are used);<br /><br />5.	Analyse the discussion by ordering the issues to throw maximum light on the subject.<br /><br />The authors stress the need for dialectical objectivity throughout; that is, the reader is only expected to arrange and present the arguments so as to present an ordered discussion <i>without taking sides</i>. So the aim of syntopical reading is to “clear away the deadwood and prepare the way for an original thinker to make a breakthrough”.<br /><br /><br />***<br /><br /><br />Whoever has read through this review so far would be asking (him/her)self: “But that’s applicable to expository books, where the main aim is the dissemination of information? What about fiction? What about poetry? What about drama?” Well, the authors extend their methodology to all kinds of books, but according to me, it falls flat. All said and done, the methodology works <i> only for expository works.</i> And that is its main problem.<br /><br />This book is not about literary theory or criticism: nor is it about literature appreciation. <i>It is a self-help book on the lines of those on time management, attending interviews, etc.</i> It outlines a methodology, the diligent following of which will guarantee results, according to its authors. It well may, for the major part of the book devoted to analytical reading gave me some insights on how to tackle books on difficult subjects like philosophy and political theory (the two stars are for that). But the book is extremely boring, and the authors’ insistence on applying their favourite methodology to all sorts of books was stretching things a bit (moreover, it takes all the fun out of reading!). And syntopic reading may make sense to an undergraduate preparing a dissertation, but is of little use to anybody else.<br /><br />If anyone wants to read this book, <i>I would recommend an inspectional reading concentrating mainly on the methodology of analytical reading only.</i> The other parts are not worth the time spent on it.<br /><br />I purchased a copy, but the book seems to be available free on the net (no idea about copyright issues!), so go ahead and try it if you want. Statutory warning: boredom ahead.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3324895668215847918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_13747830" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13747830" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/743812-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13747830?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/743812-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/743812-paul?shelf=education">education</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13747830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6368893741238895627">Probably one of the most important books you can read. I outlined the first three levels of reading a while back and I saved it. I'll post that for my "review."<br/><br/><u>How To Read A Book:</u><br/><br/><br/>(This is an outline of part of Mortimer J. Adler and Charles Van Doren’s excellent book, "How To Read A Book." The outline takes one up to the third level of reading - analytical reading. There is a fourth level, syntopical reading, but most of the intended readers of this outline, and your every day reader, does not re</span>
  <span id="freeText6368893741238895627" style="display:none">Probably one of the most important books you can read. I outlined the first three levels of reading a while back and I saved it. I'll post that for my "review."<br /><br /><u>How To Read A Book:</u><br /><br /><br />(This is an outline of part of Mortimer J. Adler and Charles Van Doren’s excellent book, "How To Read A Book." The outline takes one up to the third level of reading - analytical reading. There is a fourth level, syntopical reading, but most of the intended readers of this outline, and your every day reader, does not read syntopically. Furthermore, mastering levels 1-3 will improve what you get out of your reading 10 fold. It is sufficient to make you a very proficient reader. Also, syntopical reading is for many books, analytical reading is for one book. So, technically, the title of this post implies an an analytic outline. A syntopical outline would be titled, “How To Read Book<i>s</i>.” For these reasons I have only focused on levels 1-3. I hope the below outline will provide you with some practical knowledge of how to read well, not necessarily be well read. I also would obviously recommend purchasing Adler and Van Doren's book, "How To Read A Book," for your own library.)<br /><br />I. Be a demanding reader. Reading, if you’re going to learn anything or gain enlightenment, must be active. The more active the reader is, the better.<br /><br />A. You can be active by paying attention and focusing.<br /><br />B. By taking notes, highlighting key points and arguments, asking questions of the author, etc.<br /><br />C. Following rules for reading and making the following of these rules habitual.<br /><br />D. The demanding reader should be asking these 4 questions of the book:<br /><br />1. What is the book about as a whole? This should be stated succinctly.<br /><br />2. What is being said in detail and how? You should know the main assertions and arguments which constitute the author’s message<br /><br />3. Is the book true in whole or in part? Once you have understood the book you are obligated to make a judgment regarding it. Make up your own mind.<br /><br />4. What of it? (4) is asking things like: <br /><br />(a) How should I then live in light of what I’ve learned? <br /><br />(b) What should I do with this knowledge?<br /><br />II. The first level of reading is the reading at the basic, or elementary school, level.<br /><br />III. The second level of reading is called “inspectional reading.” This comes in two parts:<br /><br />A. Systematic skimming or pre-reading.<br /><br />1. This is achieved by: reading the title, table of contents, preface, editors note, introduction, back flap, etc.<br /><br />2. Reading the index to see the major themes, topics, ideas, and terms the author will be discussing.<br /><br />3. Reading through the book by reading the first couple of pages or so, the last couple of pages or so, and then flipping through the book, dipping in here and there.<br /><br />B. Superficial reading is the second part of inspectional reading. To achieve this you must read through the entire book at a fast pace and without stopping to think about terms you’re unfamiliar with, ideas you don’t immediately grasp, and points which are footnoted for further inspection. Doing both (A) and (B) will prepare you to read the book through for the second time; the analytical stage.<br /><br />IV. The third stage of reading is called “analytical reading.” There are three stages, made up of various rules, of analytical reading.<br /><br />A. Stage one: Rules for finding out what the book is about.<br /><br />1. Classify the book according to kind and subject matter. This is also referred to as pigeonholing a book.<br /><br />(a) Is it a poem, play, epic, work of philosophy or theology, history, science, etc.<br /><br />(b) Is it theoretical or practical.<br /><br />(i) A theoretical book reports facts, offers detached arguments, or offers insight or understanding of a position. These books teach you that something is the case.<br /><br />(ii) A practical book tells you how to live, or how to do something. These books teach you how to do something.<br /><br />(iii) As an aside, these two cannot be sharply separated. As John Frame points out in The Doctrine of God, facts and application of the facts go hand in hand. When I learn the 6th commandment I know how to apply it. But as I apply it to more diverse areas of life, I learn more about the 6th commandment.<br /><br />2. Succinctly state what the book is about. That is, find the main theme or point of the book. You should be able to state this in a sentence, paragraph at most. This is different than (IV.A.1) in that here we are asking what the book is about, not what kind of book it is.<br /><br />3. Outline the book. See this outline for an instantiation of this rule. Basically, you want to get at the bones of the book. The basic structure. The construction of the major themes and arguments. How the book proceeds. The skeleton. <br /><br />4. Define the problem(s) the author has tried to solve. To see the unity of a book you need to know why it has the unity it has (supposing it’s a good book and it has a unity!). To know why it has the unity it has you should know the authors main problem(s) he’s trying to answer; as well as subordinate questions and answers.<br /><br />B. Stage two: Rules for interpreting the book’s content.<br /><br />5. Coming to terms with the author.<br /><br />(a) A term is not a word. A term is the meaning of a word. Water and agua are two different words, they mean the same thing though.<br /><br />(b) To know the authors terms, then, is to understand the meaning of his argument or explanation, etc.<br /><br />(c) Find the important words and through them come to terms with the author.<br /><br />(d) The words he uses in an important way, or the ones you have trouble understanding, are probably the important terms you need to know.<br /><br />(e) Read all the words in context to find the meaning of the terms; how the author means them, that is. <br /><br />6. Grasp the leading propositions by finding the key sentences.<br /><br />(a) Propositions are the meanings of sentences.<br /><br />(b) You find the leading propositions by finding the key sentences.<br /><br />(c) You find the key sentences myriad ways:<br /><br />(i) The author marks them out for you in some way.<br /><br />(ii) These are the sentences that give you the most trouble.<br /><br />(iii) The sentences express judgments, I.e., they are not questions or exclamations!<br /><br />(iv) These are his reasons for affirming or denying the main problem(s) he has set out to answer.<br /><br />7. Find the author’s argument by finding them in the key sequences of sentences.<br /><br />(a) Sting together the important propositions into an ordered structure.<br /><br />(b) An argument must involve more than one statement.<br /><br />(c) An argument might be an inductive or deductive one.<br /><br />(d) Observe what the author says he must prove and what he must assume.<br /><br />8. Find which problem(s) the author solved and which one’s he did not. If he did not, find out if he knows that he did not.<br /><br />(a) Did the author solve the problem(s) he set out to solve?<br /><br />(b) Did he raise new ones in the process?<br /><br />(c) Did the author admit or know that he failed to solve some of the problem(s)?<br /><br />(d) If you know the solutions to the problem/s you can be confident that you understand the book.<br /><br />C. Stage three: Rules for criticizing a book as a communication of knowledge. You are required to criticize the book you read. You owe the author that. Criticize, or offering a judgment, does not necessarily mean that you disagree with the author. You can offer the judgment that you agree with him, you have learned something, and he has answered what he set out to. If you disagree, which is your right, be sure you have completed the above steps. You cannot critique that which you do not understand.<br /><br />9. General maxims for intellectual etiquette.<br /><br />(a) Do not begin criticism until you have completed your outline and interpretation of the book.<br /><br />(b) Do not agree disputatiously or contentiously.<br /><br />(c) Demonstrate you understand the difference between knowledge and mere opinion by giving reasons for your judgments (criticisms).<br /><br />10. Special criteria for points of criticism.<br /><br />(a) Show where the author is uninformed. This is where he lacks some piece of knowledge that is relevant to the problem(s) he was trying to solve.<br /><br />(b) Show where the author is misinformed. This is when the author asserts what is not the case.<br /><br />(c) Show where the author is illogical. Here the author’s reasoning is faulty. He has either made a non sequitur or was inconsistent.<br /><br />(d) Show where the author’s analysis, argument, or solution to problem(s) is incomplete. This is to say the author did not solve all the problems he started out to solve or did not make good use of the material at his disposal, that he failed to take into account all the ramifications, or made distinctions relevant to his undertaking.<br /><br /><br />The above outline provides the rules and strategies required for reading well. Many folks are well read, not many read well. Thomas Hobbes once said, “If I read as many books as most men do, I would be as dull-witted as they are.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="6368893741238895627" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_779990256" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/779990256" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/779990256?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=lit-crit">lit-crit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=overrated">overrated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer779990256" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2893301308296181151"><b>Who This Book is (not) For</b><br/><br/>It focuses mainly on reading expositional, rather than imaginative material. It was written in 1940, and revised in 1972, though it looks and feels more like a 40s book. <br/><br/>I read it in the hope of becoming a more analytical reader who could go on to write more coherent, concise, and original reviews. It didn’t help.<br/><br/>This may once have been a good book. Had I read it as an undergraduate, I may even have found bits of it slightly useful. As a middle-aged fiction reader in th</span>
  <span id="freeText2893301308296181151" style="display:none"><b>Who This Book is (not) For</b><br /><br />It focuses mainly on reading expositional, rather than imaginative material. It was written in 1940, and revised in 1972, though it looks and feels more like a 40s book. <br /><br />I read it in the hope of becoming a more analytical reader who could go on to write more coherent, concise, and original reviews. It didn’t help.<br /><br />This may once have been a good book. Had I read it as an undergraduate, I may even have found bits of it slightly useful. As a middle-aged fiction reader in the 21st century, I found it infuriating, boring, and mostly irrelevant. <br /><br /><b>Types of Reading</b><br /><br />There are four levels of reading: <br />1. Elementary (learning to decode the symbols).<br />2. Inspectional (time-limited skimming).<br />3. Analytical.<br />4. Syntopical (comparing and drawing conclusions). <br />I used Inspectional for most of the book - because my patience and interest were severely, and increasingly, limited.<br /><br />It focuses mainly on analytical reading of non-fiction: knowing what sort of book it is, having an idea of the content and structure etc. Its own structure is very poor. For example, four rules of analytical reading are spread across two chapters, and only listed together at the end of the second. Then, in the next chapter, you discover rule five, and six… It turns out there are 15 (yes, 15!) rules of analytical reading. Enough to put me off reading altogether.<br /><br />There are a couple of chapters devoted to fiction, but I didn’t find them helpful or insightful.<br /><br /><b>Example of Annoyances</b><br /><br />“<i>Most plays are not worth reading… because they are incomplete.</i>”<br />Sweeping generalisation followed by a non-sequitur. I rarely read plays precisely because they were written for performance, and I can’t do that effectively in my head. It does not mean that most plays are not worth reading, though.<br /><br />“<i>An author uses most words as m<b>e</b>n ordinarily do in conversation.</i>”<br />I nearly threw the book across the room, though that was probably an overreaction, born of my mounting dislike. Yes, I know it was written when it was more common to use male pronouns as generic ones, and to use “man” to mean “mankind/humankind”. But it was revised in 1972, and “m<b>e</b>n” grates far more than “m<b>a</b>n”: surely “people” would be more natural, even back then? <br /><br /><b>The Literary Canon (only one?!)</b><br /><br />I don’t think the authors really know who their audience is - a fatal flaw in any writer/reader relationship. There are constant assumptions that the reader is familiar with the classical Western canon, from ancient civilisations, through to the start of the twentieth century: Homer’s Ulysses, though to Joyce’s Ulysses. If you’d read them in school (as the authors expect), you’d either have understood them and so have little need of this book, or not understood them, and have no intention of reading this book.<br /><br />This is reflected in the impressive and somewhat daunting reading list. It explicitly includes only Western works because:<br />1.	The authors admit they know very little about Indian, Chinese, Japanese and other literary traditions. (They could have consulted someone else.)<br />2.	Apparently, there is not a single tradition in Eastern literature, as there is in Western. (I’m not sure I understand the truth or untruth of that.)<br />3.	It’s better to really know your own culture’s canon before branching out to others. (I don’t agree, but it is a valid and somewhat interesting opinion.)<br /><br /><b>Exercises</b><br /><br />An appendix has a lot of comprehension exercises (I’m not sure what term is used outside the UK). I didn’t do any of them. I’d rather read a good book.<br /><br /><b>If you want to read a book, I suggest you read a book. <br />But probably not this one.<br />If you want exercises, make it a large, heavy one!</b><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2893301308296181151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14689934537812755470">It’s such a dinosaur. Cranky, snooty, stuffy, pedantic, often condescending. It’s a manual. For intelligent reading. Very textbook-y, very fundamental. Very practical. Like some invisible ruler cracked against my keyboard-clobbering knuckles, like a pesky voice in your head.<br/><br/>It’s like having tea with your cane-thumping retiree-professor of a great-grandfather. Him demanding why you aren’t wearing hose, and will you please stand up straight? You bide your time, you promised you’d keep him company.</span>
  <span id="freeText14689934537812755470" style="display:none">It’s such a dinosaur. Cranky, snooty, stuffy, pedantic, often condescending. It’s a manual. For intelligent reading. Very textbook-y, very fundamental. Very practical. Like some invisible ruler cracked against my keyboard-clobbering knuckles, like a pesky voice in your head.<br /><br />It’s like having tea with your cane-thumping retiree-professor of a great-grandfather. Him demanding why you aren’t wearing hose, and will you please stand up straight? You bide your time, you promised you’d keep him company. And then, hours later, you realize you’re growing fond of the old coot, you can’t help but enjoy the starchiness. And there are rewards, there are gems your heart could ping with, the occasional moments of, egad, tenderness. Just imagine Gramps lecturing you on all the misreading you’ve committed, giving you precise directions on how to analyze a given book’s title, teaching you how to skim the right way. And then him suddenly going quiet, when you’ve mustered the courage to ask about fiction—him quiet and then, and then: “We do not know, we cannot be sure, that the real world is good. But the world of a great story is somehow good. We want to live there as often and as long as we can.” And you both reach for your cups of tea.</span>

<div id="review_159774400" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159774400" />
    <a title="Dante" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/726059-dante"><img alt="Dante" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573868141p2/726059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/159774400?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dante" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dante" href="/user/show/726059-dante">Dante</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dante by: </span>
      Doug Geivett
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/726059-dante?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/726059-dante?shelf=practical">practical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/726059-dante?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/726059-dante?shelf=expository">expository</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/726059-dante?shelf=reference">reference</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/726059-dante?shelf=reading">reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer159774400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2041746948399442766">I'm reading this awesome book again. I'll be writing my notes for each chapter below (It will be like a "running account" of my summary of and thoughts about every chapter). So, be warned, this is going to be a very, very, <i>very</i> long review. I hope I'll be able to write a shorter version after I'm done with the book.<br/><br/><b>Overview</b><br/><br/>Basically, How to Read a Book is a practical book. It aims to help people become intelligent readers. To read intelligently means to read actively. To read actively means to r</span>
  <span id="freeText2041746948399442766" style="display:none">I'm reading this awesome book again. I'll be writing my notes for each chapter below (It will be like a "running account" of my summary of and thoughts about every chapter). So, be warned, this is going to be a very, very, <i>very</i> long review. I hope I'll be able to write a shorter version after I'm done with the book.<br /><br /><b>Overview</b><br /><br />Basically, How to Read a Book is a practical book. It aims to help people become intelligent readers. To read intelligently means to read actively. To read actively means to read skillfully. This means that reading is actually a skill (in the same way that writing is a skill). It is an activity. Therefore, it is never passive. And, to read skillfully means to read not for information and amusement but for understanding. The authors propose that, in order to achieve this aim (intelligent, active, skillful reading), readers must observe certain rules. These rules are discussed in detail throughout the book.<br /><br />The book has 4 parts and 21 chapters. Part 1 (The Dimensions of Reading) talks about the nature and levels of reading. Part 2 (The Third Level of Reading: Analytical Reading) talks about what analytical reading is, how to go about reading a book analytically, and the general questions you must ask or the general rules you must observe when reading a book analytically. Part 3 (Approaches to Different Kinds of Reading Matter) talks about, well, the different approaches to different kinds of literature: expository books, imaginative literature, etc. Part 4 (The Ultimate Goals of Reading) talks about the fourth and highest level of reading -- syntopical reading.<br /><br /><b>Part One: The Dimensions of Reading</b><br /><br /><b>Chapter One: The Activity and Art of Reading</b><br /><br />Adler and Van Doren says that reading is an activity. Therefore, reading is active, not passive. He gives an analogy -- baseball. Reading is like "catching" the ball in baseball. It is an active thing. And because it is active, it requires skill. This book aims to help readers develop that very skill. <br /><br />Adler says that there are different goals of reading -- information, amusement/ entertainment, and understanding. This book is mainly concerned with the latter goal. So, the goal of this book is to help readers learn how to read for increased understanding. That means to read in order to move from understanding less to understanding more. That also means reading in order to become wise or enlightened.<br /><br />The authors also differentiate between reading for information and amusement, and reading for increased understanding and enlightenment. On the one hand, you are reading for information when, after reading the book, you are only able to state the facts in the book. On the other hand, you are reading for increased understanding and enlightenment when, after finishing the book, you can state the things in the book <i>and at the same time</i> explain what they mean.<br /><br />Adler and Van Doren says that books are like absent teachers. Books can teach us something (they can help us increase our understanding about the world) although their authors may no longer be physically present. That's great news, because that means that we have access to the greatest minds in the history of civilization!<br /><br />Adler and Van Doren says that the goal of this book is to help readers learn the skills they need in order to become well-read, as opposed to being merely widely-read.<br /><br /><b>Thoughts:</b><br /><br />I love Adler's baseball analogy of reading:<br /><br />Pitcher/ hitter = Writer/ author<br />Catcher = Reader<br />Ball = The ideas or information contained in the book<br /><br />I also like to be reminded that reading (at least, reading for increased understanding, which is the main goal of this book) is never passive. Reading is active -- it is an activity. That is, it involves the performance of certain mental acts. And you shouldn't take it for granted. When you read a book, you must allow it to influence or affect you. <br /><br />However, I'm not sure if I agree with the authors when they say that our goal, if we wish to become intelligent and skillful readers, is to read difficult books so that our understanding about things will increase. I mean, can we not read books that are entertaining (and therefore easy to read) but can also increase our understanding about life and the world?<br /><br />I love the idea about books being "absent teachers"! That's an awesome thought, isn't it? That means that, as readers, we still have access to the greatest minds in human history! We can still "approach" them and allow them to teach us, even if they are no longer with us physically. We can "go to" Plato, Aristotle or Aquinas and "sit at their feet" while they "lecture" us about their philosophy.<br /><br />Lastly, I love Adler's distinction between being well-read and being widely-read. I agree with him that our goal should be to become well read and not merely widely-read. <br /><br /><b>Chapter two: The Levels of Reading</b><br /><br />Adler and Van Doren talks about the different levels of reading:<br /><br />1. Elementary reading<br />2. Inspectional reading<br />3. Analytical reading<br />4. Syntopical reading<br /><br />Elementary reading asks the question, "What is the sentence saying, and what do the words mean?" Inspectional reading asks, "What is the book about as a whole? What is its structure? What are its parts?" Analytical reading asks, "What is the author saying? What does he mean? What are his arguments? Are they true? So what?" And syntopical reading asks, "Given all these books/ literature about this particular topic or issue, what analysis or conclusion can I make?"<br /><br />These levels are cumulative, so a reader cannot master the highest level of reading (syntopical reading) without first mastering elementary, inspectional, and analytical reading.<br /><br /><b>Thoughts:</b><br /><br />I like how the authors break down the skill of reading into levels. It's very helpful.<br /><br />Our ultimate goal should be syntopical reading. <br /><br /><b>Chapter three: The First Level of Reading: Elementary Reading</b><br /><br />Basically, Adler and Van Doren says that elementary reading has four stages: reading readiness, word mastery or the ability to understand basic words, rapid growth of vocabulary, and the further refinement of these skills.<br /><br />A child has to go through each of the above stages in order to master this reading level. This does not happen quickly. In fact, it takes years of practice. It starts during nursery or thereabouts, when the child becomes ready physically and intellectually to read. Then the child goes through his elementary years and learns to read basic books. During these years, the child's vocabulary grows and he begins to develop his understanding of context. Then, during his high school years, he further develops and refines his reading skills.<br /><br />Ideally, by the time the child reaches high school, he should be able to read books analytically.<br /><br /><b>Thoughts:</b><br /><br />I can honestly say that I haven't yet really mastered this basic reading level. My vocabulary is really not that wide or deep, and sometimes I find it hard to understand the context of a given sentence, especially if the book I'm reading is advanced or tertiary-level. <br /><br /><b>Chapter four: The Second Level of Reading: Inspectional Reading</b><br /><br />Adler and Van Doren talks about the second level of reading -- inspectional reading. Inspectional reading involves two steps: systematic skimming or pre-reading and superficial reading.<br /><br />Systematic skimming involves several steps: <br /><br />-- Look at the book's title and subtitle (if any);<br />-- Read the preface;<br />-- Look at the table of contents;<br />-- Look at the index; take note of the topics and authors discussed in the book;<br />-- Read the summary at the end of the book or at the end of each chapter;<br />-- Read the first few lines of each opening paragraph of every chapter;<br />-- Read the publisher's blurb.<br /><br />Superficial reading involves browsing the pages of the book slowly but superficially -- scanning every page casually.<br /><br />Adler and Van Doren says that inspectional reading achieves two things: It helps you know whether the book is, for you personally, worthy of being read analytically or not; and, it gives you a general idea of the book which is useful for your future reference.<br /><br />The authors say that there is really no such thing as a standard reading speed. Ideally, you should simply adjust your speed according to the book's difficulty.<br /><br />They also talk about reading fixations and regressions -- people's tendency to not read the book straight through without interruptions. They say these two things harm our reading because they prevent us from understanding the gist of the book. They suggest that we should use "markers" or "pointers" when we read -- this can be a pen or our finger. This increases our reading speed and comprehension significantly. Also, they say that we don't have to understand everything about the book right away. What's important is that we continue reading (without fixations and regressions) and make an effort to understand the essence of the book even if we don't understand what the author is saying 100%.<br /><br /><b>Thoughts:</b><br /><br />I love this reading level! Basically, the idea here is that not all books that are available out there deserve to be read analytically. Majority of them are worth an inspectional reading only. And inspectional reading is very, very useful. If you follow its steps, you will have a general idea of what the book is about -- you'll know what kind of a book it is (whether it's a work of fiction or non-fiction, etc.), what its subject matter is, what its structure/outline is, and what its main arguments are.<br /><br />Also, when you read a book inspectionally, you will be in a better position to decide whether the book is really that interesting or relevant for you and whether it is really worthy of your time and effort to read analytically, or whether you should just set it aside for future reference.<br /><br />Adler and Van Doren's suggestion to use the finger as a "pointer" while reading is also very helpful. <br /><br /><b>Chapter five: How to Be a Demanding Reader</b><br /><br />I think this chapter is a preparation for analytical reading, which is discussed in part 2. <br /><br />Adler and Van Doren says that in order to become an intelligent or skilled reader, you must be demanding in your reading. That is to say, you should make the effort to read and understand what you're reading. You must be motivated by the desire to enlighten yourself, to increase your understanding about matters.<br /><br />Furthermore, to become a demanding or active reader, you must ask questions while you read. What sorts of questions?<br /><br />These four, generally:<br /><br />1. What is the book about as a whole?<br />2. What is being said in detail and how?<br />3. Is it true?<br />4. What of it?<br /><br />These are also the four questions you ask when you are reading a book analytically. They are applicable to any type of book (fiction or non-fiction), but when it comes to works of imaginative literature, like novels, poems, or plays, these four questions are altered a bit.<br /><br />Of course, you shouldn't simply ask these questions, you must also do your best to answer them. The first question helps you know the book's type and subject matter. The second question helps you know the book's structure, outline, and its main parts and arguments. The third question helps you know whether the author is right or not, or whether his arguments are true or not. And the fourth question helps you know what the book's significance and implication is to your life.<br /><br />Adler and Van Doren also says that we must make the book "our own". When we buy a book, it doesn't automatically become ours. That is just the first step. The second step is to read the book and "interact" with the author's ideas by writing on the book or making marks on its significant sentences or paragraphs.<br /><br />The authors also say that, basically, in order to develop the skill of intelligent reading, you must ask questions and obey those four general rules. Rules are necessarily because they give us structure and help us discipline our reading. At first, remembering and observing these rules may be very challenging, but that is just normal. Any skill is difficult to learn at first, but with habit it becomes easier.<br /><br /><b>Thoughts:</b><br /><br />I loved this chapter!<br /><br />Basically, the above four questions lie at the very heart of analytical reading. That is, when we read a book analytically, we always ask those four questions and do our best to answer them.<br /><br />I love the idea of "making a book our own". That's very true. We shouldn't fear marking our book. We must "converse" with the author's ideas. The more we do this, the more the knowledge and insights will stick to us, so that, after answering all those questions at the end of the book, a part of us is already in the book, and a great part of the book is now in us (or, in our minds, at least).<br /><br />I just find the four questions very helpful. They guide me and provide structure to my reading. Also, they remind me that books are very, very important. Essential, even. So we shouldn't take them for granted. We shouldn't read them casually, especially if we're reading for understanding. We should allow them to influence and affect us. For example, after reading an apologetic book like Reasonable Faith by the Christian philosopher William Lane Craig, we shouldn't just set it aside and act as if nothing happened and nothing changed. We should instead ask ourselves, "Is what William Lane Craig saying true? Are his arguments really good? Does God really exist? If so, what are its implications to my life? What is its significance? What part of my mindset, mentality, philosophy, or worldview should I change, as a result of agreeing or disagreeing with Dr. Craig?" </span>
  <a data-text-id="2041746948399442766" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1088383004" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1088383004" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1088383004?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=education">education</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=help-me-help-myself">help-me-help-myself</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1088383004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5076326705911224866"><blockquote>
  <i>The person who says he knows what he thinks but cannot express it usually does not know what he thinks.</i>
</blockquote><br/>I had a lot of fun holding this book upside-down on the subway with a puzzled look on my face.<br/>		<br/>For much of his remarkably long life, Mortimer Adler was the leading proponent of the ‘Great Books’ paradigm of education. Under his leadership, the Encyclopedia Britannica published the 54-volume <i>Great Books of the Western World</i> (1952) as well as the <i>Gateway to the Great Books</i> (1963)—which, cons</span>
  <span id="freeText5076326705911224866" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>The person who says he knows what he thinks but cannot express it usually does not know what he thinks.</i>
</blockquote><br />I had a lot of fun holding this book upside-down on the subway with a puzzled look on my face.<br />		<br />For much of his remarkably long life, Mortimer Adler was the leading proponent of the ‘Great Books’ paradigm of education. Under his leadership, the Encyclopedia Britannica published the 54-volume <i>Great Books of the Western World</i> (1952) as well as the <i>Gateway to the Great Books</i> (1963)—which, considering their bulk, price, and difficulty, were surprisingly successful projects. In addition to his editing and publishing work, Adler wrote many popular books on philosophy and education. Nowadays, however, he is primarily remembered for this how-to book on reading.<br />		<br />Although I have been, and continue to be, somewhat critical of the ‘Great Book’s paradigm, I have submitted myself wholeheartedly to it. Ever since I encountered the list of the <i>Great Books of the Western World</i>, I have been gradually making my way through them. It was this list that prompted me to take my self-education more seriously; and through it, I’ve had some of my most rewarding reading experiences. Adler has thus already, albeit indirectly, exerted a huge influence on my reading life, so it seemed appropriate that I pick up his book on reading and encounter his thoughts for myself.<br />		<br />Adler promises to aid the reader of any type of reading material; but as he later admits, the strategies he suggests are most directly applicable to non-fiction. To this end, he divides reading into four levels—elementary, inspectional, analytical, and syntopical. The vast majority of Adler's exposition is focused on the third level, analytical reading. These are the strategies that allow you to get the most out of any given book.<br />		<br />Adler’s favorite philosopher was Aristotle, and it shows. Just as Aristotle’s treatises on can often seem like organized common sense, so Adler’s advice often sounds platitudinous. How do you get the most out of a book? Outline the text, be active, ask questions, pay attention to the author’s terminology, scrutinize their arguments, be aware of the difference between opinion and fact. All of this is reasonable and good advice; but it can be deflating to see that Adler’s strategies are already commonly known and widely used. At the very least, I cannot say there is anything strikingly original in here.<br />		<br />There is much to irritate in this book. For one, although it was substantially revised in 1972, Adler retains the masculine pronoun for general statements. He even says “men” when he means “people,” which can’t help but bother the contemporary ear. More importantly, Adler’s writing style is dry and wordy. He gives lengthy, verbose descriptions of simple concepts, and tends to repeat himself. One would think that this is due to his attempt to appeal to novice readers; but his formal tone, dense paragraphs, and schoolmasterly attitude will, I suspect, put off any but the bravest neophyte.<br />		<br />The most important shortcoming, I think, is the absence of the <i>why?</i> of reading at the expense of the <i>how?</i> This is curious, considering that in his section on reading practical books, Adler has this to say:<br /><blockquote>You can see why the practical author must always be something or an orator or propagandist. Since your ultimate judgment of his work is going to turn on <i>your acceptance of the goal for which he is proposing means</i>, it is up to him to win you to his ends. To do this, he has to argue in a way that appeals to your heart as well as your mind. He may have to play on your emotions and gain direction of your will.</blockquote><br />Yet Adler includes almost no appeals to the heart. There are so many injunctions, requirements, and rules in this book that you can’t help concluding that reading is a bothersome chore. This book would have been far more effective, I think, if he had dwelt more on the joys and rewards of reading. This could have been done with a simple anecdote. He could have drawn on his own experience as a reader, or included a story from his classes. A few short examples not only would have helped to encouraged any beginners, but would probably have served to enliven the dry prose. The particular is always more memorable than the general. <br />		<br />Adler’s literary personality is also irksome. His general attitude is condescending. It’s easy to imagine him standing over you, ruler in hand, staring down his nose as you struggle with Aristotle. There are some good books and a few great ones, he thinks, and the rest is basically trash. If you really want to improve your reading, you’re going to have to read really great books—which are, of course, the books in <i>The Great Books of the Western World</i>. Coupled with this condescension is a kind of willfully old-fashioned pretence. This is signaled by his persistent use of the masculine pronoun, his creaky and dry prose, and also in his dismissal of much modern intellectual work as too specialized, too technical, or just wrong-headed.<br />		<br />All these reservations aside, I must admit that Adler basically succeeds in his goal, which is to develop a methodology for getting as much as you can from non-fiction books. In my experience, his advice is sound and solid. What is more, I also must admit that every time I've read a book on Adler’s list, I found it surpassingly excellent—even great. But I have trouble imagining myself recommending this book to an inexperienced reader, and still more trouble imagining an inexperienced reader getting through it. It is therefore most valuable as a reminder to more experienced readers to take reading seriously, to be methodical, and to treat books with the respect they deserve.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5076326705911224866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1830178662" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Supratim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21432277-supratim"><img alt="Supratim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560446219p2/21432277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1830178662?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Supratim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Supratim" href="/user/show/21432277-supratim">Supratim</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21432277-supratim?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1830178662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer107391307501586000">I heard about this book in a casual conversation and my interest was piqued. When I heard that the book instructs on analytical reading I knew I had to read it.<br/><br/>I have decided that I am not going to summarize the rules enunciated in this book. Instead I would keep my review short.<br/><br/>In the first chapter the authors have mentioned that <i> “…… this book is about the art of reading for the sake of increased understanding.” </i> The authors have clearly stated that the book intends to help people understand e</span>
  <span id="freeText107391307501586000" style="display:none">I heard about this book in a casual conversation and my interest was piqued. When I heard that the book instructs on analytical reading I knew I had to read it.<br /><br />I have decided that I am not going to summarize the rules enunciated in this book. Instead I would keep my review short.<br /><br />In the first chapter the authors have mentioned that <i> “…… this book is about the art of reading for the sake of increased understanding.” </i> The authors have clearly stated that the book intends to help people understand expository works. In simple terms the book is meant for people who read serious non-fiction. However, the authors have included sections on how to read fiction, plays and poetry as well. <br /><br />The book discusses the following four levels of reading with major stress on the third type- <b><br />•	Elementary<br />•	Inspectional<br />•	Analytical<br />•	Syntopical<br /></b><br /><br />The last and most advanced level – <b> syntopical reading </b> was an added bonus. In syntopical reading the reader goes through various books on the same subject and is able to <i> "construct an analysis of the subject which may not be in any of the books." </i><br /><br />The book is good and no doubt helpful if you want to improve your reading skills. There are many tips and rules which guide you to better reading. There are separate sections on how to read practical books, imaginative literature, stories &amp; plays &amp; poetry, history, sciences &amp; mathematics, philosophy and social science. Instead of memorizing them as rule 1, rule 2 – I felt it was better to understand the gist of their advice.<br /><br />One problem with the book is that the authors were too verbose. Parts of the book were repetitive and some portions could have been pruned without affecting the quality of the book. <br /><br />I do appreciate the efforts of the authors and understand that composing such a book is not an easy task. They have done a praiseworthy job but I feel some editing would have made the book much more compact. <br /><br />The authors have included a reading list and said that these books would facilitate the growth of the mind. The list includes books on the sciences, literature, politics and statecraft, poetry, theology etc. Authors included range from ancient Greek masters to great minds of the modern world. The authors have admitted that they include books from the Asian tradition because they themselves were not <i> “particularly knowledgeable outside of the Western literary tradition” </i><br /><br />You might want to check out the reading comprehension exercises given at the end of the book. It is fun.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="107391307501586000" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText6355255447765270469" style="display:none">In junior high &amp; high school I made it my job to avoid reading altogether, just like politicians who avoid hard questions. When I was twenty I hadn't read a book since I was in fourth grade, was only partially literate, &amp; was a high school drop out with no intentions of ever cracking another book or attending another school....then I became a Christian. Jesus not only transformed my desires, habits, and life's direction; he radically transformed two things: my desire to learn and my pursuit of truth. When I came across this book I was seeking to simply become a stronger reader, but this book-outside of the Bible-has changed me more than any other. The book isn't good because itself is a wonderul read, although it is instructional; rather, it is a great book because of where it points a reader: to what the authors call the Great Conversation. I had found the Truth but had chosen to remove myself from the world of ideas &amp; people who had left a legacy of seeking the very thing that I had recently found. </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32062222?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natasha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natasha" href="/user/show/1195067-natasha">Natasha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">serious readers</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Natasha by: </span>
      Oliver DeMille
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1195067-natasha?shelf=education">education</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32062222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1955304265163022456">I read this book because I live by the mantra, "Life is Short---Read Fast" and I hoped it would teach me how to read faster. Instead it teaches you to read slower, analytically. It also teaches you how to "date" a book---to decide if you really want to spend the time to read the whole thing before commiting yourself to it. This book has a rather pedantic tone, which makes it a little dry to plow through. But I kept at it because there were philosophical gems interspersed throughout the pages. On</span>
  <span id="freeText1955304265163022456" style="display:none">I read this book because I live by the mantra, "Life is Short---Read Fast" and I hoped it would teach me how to read faster. Instead it teaches you to read slower, analytically. It also teaches you how to "date" a book---to decide if you really want to spend the time to read the whole thing before commiting yourself to it. This book has a rather pedantic tone, which makes it a little dry to plow through. But I kept at it because there were philosophical gems interspersed throughout the pages. One of my favorite of which follows:<br /><br />“But if the book belongs to the highest class—the very small number of inexhaustible books—you discover on returning that the book seems to have grown with you. You see new things in it—whole sets of new things—that you did not see before. Your previous understanding of the book in not invalidated; it is just as true as it ever was, and in the same ways that it was true before. But now it is true in still other ways, too. . . . Since it is a really good book—a great book, as we might say— it is accessible at different levels. Your impression of increased understanding on your previous reading was not false. The book truly lifted you then. But now, even though you have become wiser and more knowledgeable, it can lift you again. And it will go on doing this until you die.” (p. 343)<br /></span>

          </span>
