<div id="review_694410161" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/694410161" />
    <a title="Ted" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7213075-ted"><img alt="Ted" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101210p2/7213075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/694410161?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ted" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ted" href="/user/show/7213075-ted">Ted</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=have">have</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=americana">americana</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=education-learning">education-learning</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=non-fiction-general">non-fiction-general</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer694410161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9782357841591518729"><b>This is a book for readers and for those who wish to become readers.</b><br/><i>so writes Mortimer Adler in his first sentence.</i><br/><br/><b>3 ½</b><br/><br/>I stopped reading this book over MD (that's 1500) days ago. Hope no one was waiting for the review. Not likely, I know.<br/><br/><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1519884478i/25141576.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><br/>Mortimer Adler (1902-2001) published this book in the early days of 1940. Before the U.S. had entered WW II. I guess at that point Americans were still concerned about <b>how</b> they should be reading books. (Adler, working at the University of Chicago, was one of the </span>
  <span id="freeText9782357841591518729" style="display:none"><b>This is a book for readers and for those who wish to become readers.</b><br><i>so writes Mortimer Adler in his first sentence.</i><br><br><b>3 ½</b><br><br>I stopped reading this book over MD (that's 1500) days ago. Hope no one was waiting for the review. Not likely, I know.<br><br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1519884478i/25141576.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>Mortimer Adler (1902-2001) published this book in the early days of 1940. Before the U.S. had entered WW II. I guess at that point Americans were still concerned about <b>how</b> they should be reading books. (Adler, working at the University of Chicago, was one of the co-founders and editors of the Great Books of the Western World project, published by Britannica in the 1950s.)<br><br>The currently available edition, shown as the cover to the book reviewed here, is based on a 1972 "heavily revised" edition, for which Charles Van Doren (born 1926) is listed as a co-author. I have no idea what exactly Van Doren contributed to the revised edition, but Adler notes that he had been working with him for many years at the Institute for Philosophical Research. So I simply use "Adler" as the author in what follows.<br><br><b>the book</b><br><br>Adler's idea behind the book was that certain books - "great" books, perhaps even <i>near great</i> books – deserved, <i>demanded</i>, to be read in a way that would allow the reader to discover and understand what the author was saying, and why it was significant. In a way, that is, that would allow the reader to enter into an internal dialogue with the author, in which the reader could see the rationale behind the author's assertions, and make an intelligent decision about whether he agreed or disagreed with the author. <br><br>There are many lists in the book. The four levels of reading (Elementary, Inspectional, Analytical, and Syntopical); lengthy, detailed descriptions of the levels; three different <i>stages</i> of analytical reading (the first, four Rules for Finding What a Book is About; the second, four rules for Finding What a Book Says; the third, <i>seven</i> for Criticizing a Book as a Communication of Knowledge (arranged in <i>two categories</i> of such rules – General Maxims of Intellectual Etiquette &amp; Special Criteria for Points of Criticism); the two types of books (practical books, imaginative literature); the two <i>kinds</i> of practical books (one type containing <i>rules</i>, the other type concerned with principles that generate rules) [this is more confusing in the telling, than this my summary indicates]; separate chapters on how to read several genres of books (stories/plays/poems, history, science/mathematics, philosophy, social science); five steps in syntopical reading. All of these things need to be read closely to know Adler's meaning, since he insists on using terms which he thinks necessary, and which he must define in detail, rather than more common distinctions which, for him, are not precise enough.<br><br>I found much that was interesting in the book; but having read through over half the chapters, and seeing what remained, I decided that the remainder of the book could be used as a reference, and abandoned the rest of it. I did enjoy Adler's proscription to underline and write in the margins, since it was a thumbs-up to what I'd been doing for a long time. I didn't enjoy, indeed grew weary of, his nearly constant use of masculine pronouns. I suspect that the extremely few feminine pronouns crept in with the revised edition I have, and were missing in the early 1940s.<br><br>Near the end of the book, Adler writes of a Pyramid of Books. Those lower in the pyramid, more than 99 per cent of the total <blockquote>will not make sufficient demands on you for you to improve your skill in reading… These are books that can be read only for amusement or information. The amusement may be of many kinds [thankfully, he doesn't enlarge on the kinds], and the information may be enlightening in all sorts of ways [ditto]. But you should not expect to learn anything of importance from them. In fact, you do not have to read them – analytically – at all. Skimming will do.</blockquote>He goes on to talk about the tippy-top of the pyramid. "Good" books, the penultimate layer, do need to be read analytically to extract what is truly worthwhile in them; however, if they are read correctly, they never need to be read again – you've got what's worthwhile, and (assuming you have a good memory) you're finished with them.<br><br>But then there's the very top layer of books, perhaps less than a hundred, that contain riches that can never be fully extracted no matter how diligently you read them, and how often you re-read them. These are <i>inexhaustible</i> books. This may sound kind of over-the-top, but I found this section sort of moving. Adler explains things well here, acknowledging that <i>for any given person</i>, of the hundred or so books in this top layer, there are only a few of that total that truly speak to the interests and the inner "soul" (my word) of that person. [And I would add, that for any given person, there are books <i>outside</i> this top layer that could be read over and over, finding new insights and new inspirations each time.]<br><br>In Appendix A, Adler presents a list of 137 authors, with one or more books for each, which he deems to be definitely "worth reading".<blockquote>Although not all of the books listed are "great" ... all of them will reward you for the effort you make to read them. All of these books are over most people's heads – sufficiently so, at any rate, to force most readers to stretch their minds to understand and appreciate them. And that, of course, is the kind of book you should seek out.</blockquote>True to form, Adler needs another three pages to qualify, and explain in detail, everything that could possibly be misconstrued, or cause a feeling of being overwhelmed, by the books in the list.<br><br>The list is arranged in chronological order, beginning with the Iliad and the Odyssey of Homer, and the New Testament. I'll give the last ten (most recent) authors here.<br><br>129. Bertrand Russell: <i>The Problems of Philosophy; The Analysis of Mind; An Inquiry into Meaning and Truth; Human Knowledge - Its Scope and Limits</i><br><br>130. Thomas Mann: <i>The Magic Mountain; Joseph and His Brothers</i><br><br>131. Albert Einstein: <i>The Meaning of Reality; On the Method of Theoretical Physics; The Evolution of Physics.</i><br><br>132. James Joyce: "The Dead" in <i>Dubliners, Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man, Ulysses</i><br><br>133. Jacques Maritain: <i>Art and Scholasticism; The Degrees of Knowledge; The Rights of Man and Natural Law; True Humanism</i><br><br>134. Franz Kafka: <i>The Trial; The Castle</i><br><br>135. Arnold Toynbee: <i>A Study of History; Civilization on Trial</i><br><br>136. Jean Paul Sarte: <i>Nausea; No Exit; Being and Nothingness</i><br><br>137. Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn: <i>The First Circle; Cancer Ward</i><br><br><br><br><br><br>The entire list is on Wiki, as part of the article on <i>How to Read a Book</i>. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/How_to_Read_a_Book#Reading_list_(1972_edition)">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/How_to_...</a>. Note that the authors mentioned are, with almost no exceptions, white males.<br><br><br><b>rating</b><br><br>I've rounded up to 4 stars. Depending a lot on the reader – how they react to Adler's very teacherly prose, how they get along with his pedantry, what exactly they hope to get from the book, how much they are used to reading in the "analytical" manner that Adler espouses – it could really be anything from a 2 to a 5. That's the range that friends on Goodreads have given it, though the average friend rates it only 3 to 3 1/2.<br><br><br><br><br>. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .<br><b>Previous review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2267684147" rel="nofollow noopener">North of Boston</a><br><b>Random review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/548827936" rel="nofollow noopener">The Life and Writings of Abraham Lincoln</a><br><b>Next review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1982578134" rel="nofollow noopener">Main Street</a><br><br><b>Previous library review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1649571609" rel="nofollow noopener">Christ and Apollo</a><br><b>Next library review:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/718427563" rel="nofollow noopener">Classics for Pleasure</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9782357841591518729" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_27775516" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27775516" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1344527-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566146813p2/1344527.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27775516?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/1344527-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780671212803" href="/book/show/1639212.How_to_Read_a_Book">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1344527-dan?shelf=books-reading-writing">books-reading-writing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27775516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1400409174718569561">I read this in 2003. Most of it was concerned with the various levels of reading - from skimming to syntopical - and the various methods and processes involved in reading at each of those levels. There was also a lengthy section on ways to approach the reading of different genres. While I found all of this interesting, I felt it was ultimately impractical for me because focusing on the method by which I read would distract from the enjoyment reading has always afforded me. I'm sure I've subconsc</span>
  <span id="freeText1400409174718569561" style="display:none">I read this in 2003. Most of it was concerned with the various levels of reading - from skimming to syntopical - and the various methods and processes involved in reading at each of those levels. There was also a lengthy section on ways to approach the reading of different genres. While I found all of this interesting, I felt it was ultimately impractical for me because focusing on the method by which I read would distract from the enjoyment reading has always afforded me. I'm sure I've subconsciously incorporated some of the things I learned from Adler and Van Doren, but I can't make myself follow a set of steps or procedures to do something I enjoy doing the way I've always done it.<br /><br />The first thing from the book that has stuck with me was a statement to the effect that, to be considered well-read, one should have at some point read <i>The Iliad</i>, <i>The Odyssey</i>, <i>The Aeneid</i>, <i>The Divine Comedy</i>, and <i>Paradise Lost</i>. I'm not sure why that stuck with me or why it then became important for me to read them. Maybe it was just another "reading list" to be conquered but the almost total absence of poetry in all my years of reading made this one a little daunting. Since 2003 I've read and enjoyed all of them, and I’ve discovered that I enjoy getting into a good epic poem. One of the benefits of having read these is that my reading of books that reference or allude to them has been enhanced.<br /><br />The second thing I took from Adler and Van Doren was their actual reading list, included as an appendix. Very heavy on the ancient classics, philosophy, and science, so working my way through it has been a lengthy and winding trail.<br /><br />The last two things are somewhat related and are those that have most impacted my reading; not how I read, but how my reading affects me. These are the concepts of the pyramid of books and the conversation of great books – what I call the literary conversation. The authors contend that the books we read form a pyramid with the base consisting of the majority of our reading material; that which doesn't stretch our reading skills. As we move up the pyramid, we encounter books that are increasingly difficult for us because, at the time we read them, our reading skills – how we understand a book and make it our own – are not at the level necessary to grasp all that is available from the book. If we return to those books at a later date they will not seem as difficult because our reading skills will have improved in the interim. At the peak of each of our individual pyramids is the handful of books that will improve our abilities each time we read them because there is much more there than can be grasped at a single skill level. This is not to say that any given book will be at the peak of everyone's pyramid. There are too many ways for books and people to relate to each other for that to ever be the case, and that's where the literary conversation comes into play.<br /><br />Complex systems have been defined as “system(s) composed of interconnected parts that as a whole exhibit one or more properties not obvious from the properties of the individual parts.” When it comes to reading, that complex system is the literary conversation. Adler and Van Doren believe that great books - those that stand the test of time and consistently stretch the reading abilities of large numbers of people - engage in a conversation with us and, through us, with each other. In my experience, the most common form of this conversation is when an author quotes or alludes to another book in his own book. The book quoted or alluded to is generally one that is considered a classic. Some instances can be fairly extensive as in Andrew Davidson's <i>The Gargoyle</i> wherein the protagonist experiences a chapter-long nightmare based on Dante's <i>The Inferno</i>. A more subtle - and to me more satisfying - form of the conversation occurs when I recognize in different authors a common theme or concept expressed from differing perspectives. I read Thomas Pynchon's <i>V.</i> and Umberto Eco's <i>Baudolino</i> about a month apart. Despite the dissimilarity of the authors and the stories, at a climactic scene in each of the books the point that, in life, the journey is often more important than the destination was expressed from almost opposite perspectives. The scene from <i>V.</i> gave me the perspective that sometimes, when what we've sought is almost within our grasp, we make our faith a lie so that we don't have to give up our quest by achieving its goal, while <i>Baudolino</i> looked at the same point from the perspective that the achievement of a goal sometimes precipitates the loss of the spirit, drive, and fellowship that were the quest. I don't know if I happen to periodically recognize such similarities because of where I am in life at the time or if Serendipity flutters her wings at just the right moment, but they always elicit from me a softly uttered, "Wow!"<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1400409174718569561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_778608657" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/778608657" />
    <a title="Margitte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte"><img alt="Margitte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361772481p2/7481492.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/778608657?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margitte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margitte" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte">Margitte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer778608657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13554892548140698147"><br/>I have been reading this book very slowly, on and off, the past two months, trying to have enough time to concentrate, focus, be analytical, critical and syntopical. <br/><br/>This pedagogical work is so comprehensive, it will take forever to summarize the content. In short, this book is a must-read for any serious reader of the GREAT BOOKS of all times.<br/><br/> It can be regarded as a manual for lecturers/teachers/reviewers, or anybody who needs to discuss a serious book. Book clubs comes to mind here for those </span>
  <span id="freeText13554892548140698147" style="display:none"><br />I have been reading this book very slowly, on and off, the past two months, trying to have enough time to concentrate, focus, be analytical, critical and syntopical. <br /><br />This pedagogical work is so comprehensive, it will take forever to summarize the content. In short, this book is a must-read for any serious reader of the GREAT BOOKS of all times.<br /><br /> It can be regarded as a manual for lecturers/teachers/reviewers, or anybody who needs to discuss a serious book. Book clubs comes to mind here for those of us who do not need to face a classroom and would want to apply the knowledge somewhere. You can find an interesting synopsis of the author's ideas here: <b>
  <a href="https://docs.google.com/document/d/19c-zXLAvlRePCudHD0wMaAMeAegXw58MHFtTOLvmKQQ/edit" rel="nofollow noopener">GOOGLE DOCS</a>
</b><br /><br />You will immediately recognize many familiar aspect in this reading sheet compiled from Adler's book. After finishing the book you will understand why it is imperative to become an <i>active, skillful reader</i>. The important aspect of reading is not to read as many books as you possibly can, but rather read each book really well and enjoy it more. <br /><br />Of course, while reading it, I was making notes as usual - some were comments, others were highlights of the content. So instead of repeating the entire book here, I will briefly share my interpretation there of.<br /><br />The original book was written in 1940, with recent updates made to suit modern audiences. Someone should read both versions and report the differences. That's to say, someone who has the time to do that! <br /><br />The author proposes that books should be read three times for different reasons: <b>
  <i>analytically, interpretively, and critically</i>
</b>.<blockquote>I. The first reading can be called structural or analytic. Here the reader proceeds from the whole to its parts.<br /><br />II. The second reading can be called interpretative or synthetic. Here the reader proceeds from the parts to the whole.<br /><br />III. The third reading can be called critical or evaluative. Here the reader judges the author, and decides whether he agrees or disagrees.</blockquote>I was thinking about the information-overload, the dumping of reading material onto carricula and how students struggle to keep ahead. We all know that it leads to superficial reading, not because the student does not have the mental ability to understand or analyze, but simply because modern students do not have the time to do so. <br /><br />No wonder students leave books behind the day they leave the torture chambers of education! What could have been an introduction to intellectual freedom, becomes a dreaded memory of concentration camps! (pun intended). So in effect our education authorities are killing off the desire for learning. It's in the book as well, and not only my opinion! <blockquote>
  <i>“Do not train a child to learn by force or harshness; but direct them to it by what amuses their minds, so that you may be better able to discover with accuracy the peculiar bent of the genius of each.” - Plato</i>
</blockquote> In the reading milieu, such as Goodreads, where readers gather to discuss books, time has become a lesser evil, since passion for reading replaces more or less the urgent need for gathering information - or writing dissertations, and enjoyment has become a major factor. Yes, then it will be possible to read a book three times, provided you don't have a TBR-Bucket list that is slowly driving you bonkers!<br /><br />There are different types of books, according to the author:<br /><b>I. Digests and repetitions of other books;<br />II. Original communications</b><br /><br />The authors propose that the original communications by, for instance, Plato and Dante, be read to enable the reader to form a personal opinion of the original author's books. <br /><br /><i>Digests and Repetitions of other books</i>, according to him, leaves too much room for subjective interpretation in which much of the original charm may get lost. However, in my opinion, most readers today are dependent on the translations to enjoy the books. In fact, if it wasn't for the translations, most of these books would never have enjoyed such a wide audience today. As discussed in the book, <i>Through the Language Glass: Why the World Looks Different in Other Languages</i>, by Guy Deutscher, the translation in itself can alter the meaning of some words by simply interpreting the original word subjectively in a different culture. The Bible translations is a prime example of it. <br /><br />Then the interpretation of the content of a book in itself can differ vastly, as can be observed in the different reviews of one book on Goodreads alone. Classroom interpretations by teachers are not much different. But let's be honest, if it wasn't for excellent teachers introducing us to Shakespeare through manuals such as Shakespeare for Dummies, or something similar, we, as modern readers, especially the non-English speaking contingent, all might still be in therapy after colliding with this particular author head-on without any rescue workers on the way! Honestly, I am still reeling and it has already been decades later!<br /><br />However, the authors' arguments in the book, about reading the <i>original communications</i>, make sense. Perhaps different translations of the same work should be considered. It is with the <i>original communications</i> (Great books in particular) that the rules of reading can assist greatly. What a great thought to know that by reading the <i>original communications</i> from the old masters, we actually experience a direct communication with him or her! I never really thought about it this way.<br /><br /><i>How to Read a Book: The Classic Guide to Intelligent Reading</i> by Mortimer J. Adler, Charles Van Doren, is old news in a new jacket, as we say in Africa. Most of the principles in the original book have been implemented at universities and classrooms since 1940 and certainly made a huge difference. Although we did not always have time to follow the rules exactly, <b>we were taught the rules</b>, which enabled us to implement it more, later in our lives, when we had time to do so. DIY-manuals versus classroom participation is an important concept discussed in the book - an important one.<br /><br />What with reading printed books being stamped with an expiry date all over it, according to the doomsayers, we could at least attempt to teach family members the joy and secrets of reading again, after a book like this. <br /><br />For interest's sake: I recently had a conversation with a documentary-producer from China. He told me how futile our modern information technology have become. It doesn't matter how small or big the storage devices are, and will become, they still have a limited life span. Thus, everything stored digitally or electronically, and not being restored constantly on new devices, will be lost in time and ultimately proven useless. It's a sobering and upsetting thought. <br /><br />In that sense, there's still room for printed books. They might not last as long as the Egyptian papyrus scrolls, clay tablets of Mesopotamia,the Pre-Columbian codices of the Americas, and the old books preserving master texts such as the Italian <i>Divinia Commedia</i>, by Dante Alighieri, begun c. 1308 and completed 1320, but we have a chance of keeping future generations informed by keeping the art of reading printed works alive. Perhaps there's also still a need to memorize important information as was done a few thousand years ago when oral history was the only way forward! :-)<br /><br />This book enables rookies to get introduced to the real joy of real reading again. The point is, we need to read books like these again to remind us of this dying art form.<blockquote>
  <i>In the first place, you must be able to grasp what is being offered as knowledge. In the second place, you must judge whether what is being offered is really acceptable to you as knowledge. In the other words, there is first the task of understanding the book, and second the job of criticizing it. These two are quite separate, as you will see more and more.</i>
</blockquote>The text is divided into 4 sections (over 21 chapters):<br /><b>Part 1 - THE ACTIVITY OF READING</b> - The Dimensions of Reading; read to understand, not for relaxation, gaining knowledge, amusement, or anything else.;<br /><b>Part 2 - THE RULES</b> -The Third Level of Reading: the rules for analytical reading; <br /><b>Part 3 - THE REST OF THE READER'S LIFE</b> - Approaches to Different Kinds of Reading Matter;<br /><b>Part 4 - The Ultimate Goals of Reading</b> - syntopical reading.<br /><br />There are excellent reviews and discussions of this book in other Goodread reviews. You might want to read a few.<br /><br /> If you really have time to apply all the rules to a book, resulting in taking up to one month to read one book, you might want to choose a book that is really worth the effort. The GREAT books of all times will do nicely :-)) Nonfiction will work even better.<br /><br />Some hick-ups: There were copious numbers of spelling or typing errors in the book, which drove me slightly insane. A pity, really. Hopefully it was only this particular edition I have found. Quite some jumping-around on the concepts; repetitive, elaborate, tedious illustrations of the ideas; and perhaps a too formal classroom approach to the information that might scare some readers off. However, it is still a highly informative read. For a book being written in 1940 it is not too bad. I would have thought that the approach to the information should have been 'modernized' though, while it was republished. The readers of today are a totally different kettle of fish. Editing is the magic word!<br /><br />I am not sure if this is the best book to read for improving reading skills, but it is certainly the father of them all and I learnt a lot from it. I will refer back to it as well - it was worth it. It took two months, remember? :-)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13554892548140698147" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_569145958" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/569145958" />
    <a title="Diem" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1347421-diem"><img alt="Diem" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567724413p2/1347421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/569145958?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diem" href="/user/show/1347421-diem">Diem</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1347421-diem?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer569145958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18299881259987928047">This is a tremendous personal victory for me for two reasons, the first of which has to do with the book itself and the second of which has to do with a concerted reallocation of time. <br/><br/>Ever since I first learned of the book's existence I understood that it was a book that I really SHOULD read. And I had an intention to read it. A desire to read it. And yet, I never read it. I did lots of other things that could have gone without doing. So, it really wasn't a matter of time. Celebrities got fat a</span>
  <span id="freeText18299881259987928047" style="display:none">This is a tremendous personal victory for me for two reasons, the first of which has to do with the book itself and the second of which has to do with a concerted reallocation of time. <br /><br />Ever since I first learned of the book's existence I understood that it was a book that I really SHOULD read. And I had an intention to read it. A desire to read it. And yet, I never read it. I did lots of other things that could have gone without doing. So, it really wasn't a matter of time. Celebrities got fat and thin and pregnant and married and arrested and fired and would have done all of those things even if I had not been so fastidious about tracking it. <br /><br />Inspired by a listening to a Susan Wise Bauer lecture on self education, I set aside time before my morning workout to read a book. I didn't have to get up any earlier than was my habit. I just had to swap online time for book time. I set a goal of reading 4 mornings a week for a minimum of 30 minutes. I decided to start with this book because my ultimate goal was to tackle some selections from the Great Books compendium in preparation for guiding my homeschooled eldest daughter through a Great Books education. <br /><br />After the first morning, I was so invigorated by the reading that I decided not to take the next day off but to continue the reading. And I never did take a morning off after that. It has become one of the highlights of my day. I would feel deprived if I could not start my day this way. <br /><br />The book. What a revelation. I am the reader being spoken of in the places where he refers to readers that have read wide but not deep. Prolific readers who couldn't tell you what they had just read if their lives depended upon it. For shame! I took all of Adler's advice on how to REALLY read a book to heart. The trend today is for authors to begin each book that deals with how to embark upon a new venture or how to approach an old venture in a new way by saying, "This seems hard but it is really QUITE EASY!" Not Adler. Adler tells you upfront: Reading a book well is hard. It requires a great deal of effort. It will become frustrating sometimes. It will still be worth it.<br /><br />I outlined the book as I read which meant it took even longer to read but for perhaps the first time I closed a book with a sense that I truly understood it. I had not skimmed over vast sections of it with an arrogant sense of already knowing the gist of it. And now that I think about it, I've spent most of my life "gist" reading most books. <br /><br />I felt an enormous sense of relief when Adler explained that the reason why we give up on books that seem over our heads is because they ARE over our heads and it is incumbent upon us to raise ourselves to that level. I had always assumed that I should understand it and the fact that I did not meant I was too stupid to ever understand it. I would give up and read something "light" and excuse it as a "palate cleanser" (looking at you, Bridget Jones right next to the dusty and never-read Tom Jones on my shelf). <br /><br />I won't get into the mechanics of the book but to say that it is impeccably organized. The vocabulary might be challenging but don't worry. You can do this. You should do this. And there should be a class in every school that teaches this book and the approach to reading that it prescribes. Adler would not approve of my classification of his book but here it is: Game Changer. Cannot over-recommend. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18299881259987928047" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_98289297" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98289297" />
    <a title="Taka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73783-taka"><img alt="Taka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1293255339p2/73783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98289297?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Taka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Taka" href="/user/show/73783-taka">Taka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73783-taka?shelf=japan_jul07-aug11">japan_jul07-aug11</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73783-taka?shelf=other">other</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98289297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1857720490747772044">Tedious,turgid, and torturous--<br/><br/>Thank God I've gained a few insights from this: the usefulness of inspectional reading and how to read poetry (which consists of reading it as fast as you can and rereading it aloud). Some thoughts on syntopical reading are somewhat interesting for anyone writing dissertations and theses, but not really for the average reader without a Ph.D. to pursue.<br/><br/>I thank God for the insights because otherwise I would've wasted all my time. I found 90% of the information simply</span>
  <span id="freeText1857720490747772044" style="display:none">Tedious,turgid, and torturous--<br /><br />Thank God I've gained a few insights from this: the usefulness of inspectional reading and how to read poetry (which consists of reading it as fast as you can and rereading it aloud). Some thoughts on syntopical reading are somewhat interesting for anyone writing dissertations and theses, but not really for the average reader without a Ph.D. to pursue.<br /><br />I thank God for the insights because otherwise I would've wasted all my time. I found 90% of the information simply useless and terribly presented. The authors tend to painstakingly explain problems with unnecessarily long illustrations that take into account minuscule and trifling technicalities and terminological issues that make for an extremely tedious read.<br /><br />In fact, the tedium of it is simply incredible. The authors used terminology of their own and elaborated on every little detail. The book could have been dubbed, <i>Dialectical Philosophy of Analytical and Syntopical Reading</i> or some such insufferable, yawn-inducing title.<br /><br />Frankly, after page 100, I HAD to skim most of the material to stay awake and not waste any more minute of my life. <br /><br />Just know that superficial reading - reading without stopping to think or look up words - is important because it enables us to grasp the whole. This makes sense from a scientific POV since we like to start from general statements (<i>Brain Rules</i>) and when reading poetry which is usually emotional, we want our right brain to absorb the mood and emotions pervading the work (<i>Writing the Natural Way</i>).<br /><br />Don't waste your time - if you're still interested, inspectional-read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1857720490747772044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_628504827" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/628504827" />
    <a title="travelgirlut" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/184077-travelgirlut"><img alt="travelgirlut" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1262817895p2/184077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/628504827?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="travelgirlut" class="user" itemprop="url" name="travelgirlut" href="/user/show/184077-travelgirlut">travelgirlut</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/184077-travelgirlut?shelf=education">education</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer628504827" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16682259557493140894">May 26, 2013: My kids laughed at me when they saw I was getting a book about how to read a book. I'm an avid reader so I honestly didn't think I would get much from reading this, but I have to be honest, I usually don't remember much about what I read once I'm finished. This book teaches you how to get the most from books that are actually worth reading. <br/><br/>Some important points I gleaned:<br/>- Not all books are worth reading well. Some are only worthy of a cursory read-through.<br/>- A good book should mov</span>
  <span id="freeText16682259557493140894" style="display:none">May 26, 2013: My kids laughed at me when they saw I was getting a book about how to read a book. I'm an avid reader so I honestly didn't think I would get much from reading this, but I have to be honest, I usually don't remember much about what I read once I'm finished. This book teaches you how to get the most from books that are actually worth reading. <br /><br />Some important points I gleaned:<br />- Not all books are worth reading well. Some are only worthy of a cursory read-through.<br />- A good book should move us from understanding less to understanding more, not just give us new facts or knowledge.<br />- Reading is like having a conversation with the author, which is really kind of cool if you think about it. You can talk to Plato or Aristotle or even Hitler if you want, just by reading the words they put down on a page. And we should be talking back to the authors, finding the truth or fallacies in what they say, analyzing their words and coming up with our own to counter theirs.<br /><br />While this book was very strong in explaining the whys of reading better, I felt it fell a little flat in the how department, especially in the sections at the end explaining how to read specific types of books. I would wrap my brain around how reading fiction is different than non-fiction and just need a concrete example to cement it all, and it would move on to something else.<br /><br />If you wanted to pair this book with another that gives more hand-holding in the how-to department, I would read this one first, then follow it up with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/86145.The_Well_Educated_Mind_A_Guide_to_the_Classical_Education_You_Never_Had" title="The Well-Educated Mind A Guide to the Classical Education You Never Had by Susan Wise Bauer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Well-Educated Mind: A Guide to the Classical Education You Never Had</a>. The-Well Educated Mind gives very specific step-by-step instructions on the actual reading of books, but is much weaker in the why department. So between the two, you can get a complete picture of how and why this process works to get our brains moving and learning.<br /><br />Besides the actual content of the book, I found How to Read a Book to be written in such a way that I found it a pleasure to read. Something about how the authors put words together made me feel smarter just reading it. They used intelligent words and phrasing that pushed me just enough without feeling pompous. Even in the drier sections of the book, I found myself slowing down just to enjoy the words. I realize this marks me out as a bit of a word nerd, but oh well!<br /><br />UPDATE June 29, 2016: I've now read this book for a second time and still feel the same way as I did above. This time through I took notes while reading and I think I was able to get a better picture of how it all fits together, but I still think the second section that describes the differences in reading varying types of books to be weaker than the initial how and why section. The chapters actually taper off in usefulness the farther into the book you go.<br /><br />My biggest take-away from this reading: Alder tells us that if you've read a good book well, you should never have to read it again because you've garnered everything from it that you can and learned all that it has to teach you. However, there are some great books that will always have something new to teach you because they grow with you. Those second type of books are the ones we should be looking for and reading.<br /><br />I feel like I've now gotten everything from this book that it has to teach me. My task now is to take what I've learned and go use it on the great books out there in the world. <br /><br />(I again plan to follow this up with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/86145.The_Well_Educated_Mind_A_Guide_to_the_Classical_Education_You_Never_Had" title="The Well-Educated Mind A Guide to the Classical Education You Never Had by Susan Wise Bauer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Well-Educated Mind</a> since there is a new edition out and I want to see what's new!)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16682259557493140894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2708881007640601624">It changed the way I read books (I read primarily non-fiction). Reading is no longer just "look at every word until they are all seen," nor is it like a tape that plays from the beginning to the end. This book taught me the value of skimming books as a way of time management.   <span id="freeText2708881007640601624" style="display:none">It changed the way I read books (I read primarily non-fiction). Reading is no longer just "look at every word until they are all seen," nor is it like a tape that plays from the beginning to the end. This book taught me the value of skimming books as a way of time management. From this, I also became better aware of how to connect with the author of a book, through using the 'tools' the author provides to help understand the content: everything from the table of contents, to the introduction, to each paragraph, to the index. In general, this book has taught me how to approach reading such that I can benefit more from (and therefore better appreciate) a book and all the thought and effort put therein by its author.<br /><br />For more eloquent and comprehensive praise, see amazon.com's <a href="http://amzn.to/TPxbjx" rel="nofollow noopener">reviews</a>.<br /><br />Note: I get weird looks from people when they see its title. :P</span>

<div id="review_3194235388" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3194235388" />
    <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid"><img alt="Farhan Khalid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350661583p2/2881911.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3194235388?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Farhan Khalid" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid">Farhan Khalid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/49422528-how-to-read-a-book">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3194235388" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11112107143688678099">Reading can be done for three reasons: <br/><br/>Entertainment – to relax, hear a story<br/><br/>Information – to acquire facts <br/><br/>Understanding – to develop insight <br/><br/>Reading actively means mastering four levels of reading: <br/><br/>Elementary reading – Turning symbols into information<br/><br/>Inspectional reading – Getting the most from a book in a given time<br/><br/>Analytical reading – Thorough and complete reading for understanding<br/><br/>Synoptic reading – Exploring a subject through wide reading<br/><br/>Make every book you read your own. Use: <br/><br/>Highlighting </span>
  <span id="freeText11112107143688678099" style="display:none">Reading can be done for three reasons: <br /><br />Entertainment – to relax, hear a story<br /><br />Information – to acquire facts <br /><br />Understanding – to develop insight <br /><br />Reading actively means mastering four levels of reading: <br /><br />Elementary reading – Turning symbols into information<br /><br />Inspectional reading – Getting the most from a book in a given time<br /><br />Analytical reading – Thorough and complete reading for understanding<br /><br />Synoptic reading – Exploring a subject through wide reading<br /><br />Make every book you read your own. Use: <br /><br />Highlighting – underline, circle, star, asterisk and fold pages<br /><br />Linking – number arguments on the page, reference other pages or sections<br /><br />Synthesising – write in the margins, top and bottoms of pages and front and end papers<br /><br />A person tries to read something that at first he does not completely understand. The thing to be read is initially better or higher than the reader. The writer is communicating something that can increase the reader’s understanding<br /><br />Learning is meant understanding more, not remembering more information<br /><br />Enlightenment is achieved only when you know what author means and why he says it<br /><br />Active reading is the effort to understand<br /><br />One must also use one’s senses and imagination. One must observe, and remember, and construct imaginatively what cannot be observed<br /><br />Reading is learning from one who is absent<br /><br />If you ask a book a question, you must answer it yourself<br /><br />Television is so designed as to make thinking seem unnecessary<br /><br />The non-thinker: He inserts a packaged opinion into his mind. He then pushes a button and <i>plays back</i> the opinion whenever it seems appropriate to do so. He has performed acceptably without having had to think<br /><br />If you understand perfectly everything the author has to say, you have not increased your understanding<br /><br />Gradually lift yourself from a state of understanding less to one of understanding more<br /><br />You have gained nothing but information if you have exercised only your memory<br /><br />Widely read and to be well-read are not the same thing<br /><br />When the learner proceeds without the help of any sort of teacher – such learning constitute the art of unaided discovery<br /><br />Study the table of contents to obtain a general sense of the book’s structure; use it as you would a road map before taking a trip<br /><br />Check the index<br /><br />Estimate the range of topics covered<br /><br />Look now at the chapters that seem to be pivotal to its argument<br /><br />Read summary statements in their opening or closing pages<br /><br />Turn the pages, dipping in here and there, reading a paragraph or two<br /><br />Read the last two or three pages<br /><br />State the unity of the whole book in a single sentence, or at most a few sentences (a short paragraph)<br /><br />Discover its theme or main point<br /><br />Classify the book according to kind and subject matter<br /><br />Define the problem or problems the author is trying to solve<br /><br />The best poetry is that which is the most richly ambiguous<br /><br />The most important words are those that give you trouble<br /><br />You have to discover the meaning of a word you do not understand by using the meanings of all the other words in the context that you do understand<br /><br />You have to see the main sentences<br /><br />Wonder is the beginning of wisdom in learning from books as well as from nature<br /><br />State in your own words<br /><br />You often have to search through all the paragraphs of a chapter to find the sentences you can construct into a statement of a single argument<br /><br />Leave certain things unsaid<br /><br />If you find the conclusion first, then look for the reasons. If you find the reasons first, see where they lead<br /><br />Interpret author’s key words<br /><br />A word that has one meaning in everyday conversation may take on a different meaning in a book. In <i>Darwin’s Origin of Species</i>, for instance, the important words include <i>variety, selection</i> and <i>adaptation</i><br /><br />Deal with his most important sentences<br /><br />Reading a book is a kind of conversation. The reader has the last word<br /><br />Reading does not stop with understanding. It must be completed by the work of criticism, the work of judging<br /><br />Take the responsibility of taking a position<br /><br />To criticize is not always to disagree<br /><br />Regard disagreements as capable of being resolved<br /><br />Distinguish between genuine knowledge and mere opinion<br /><br />Knowledge is based on evidence <br /><br />Opinion is unsupported judgment<br /><br />Do not begin criticism until you have completed your outline and your interpretation of the book<br /><br />Measure the truth work has achieved<br /><br />A person who has read widely but not well deserves to be pitied rather than praised<br /><br />The great writers read fewer books. But what they did read, they read well. Because they had mastered these books, they became peers with their authors<br /><br />Apply the rules described in the reading<br /><br />Abstracts are useful in only two connections:<br /><br />First, they can help to jog your memory of a book’s contents, if you have already read it<br /><br />Second, when you wish to know whether a certain work is likely to be germane to your project<br /><br />An abstract can never replace the reading of a book, but it can sometimes tell you whether you want or need to read the book or not<br /><br />Have idea what you want to know from book<br /><br />We would not recommend looking up a word during your first reading<br /><br />Practical books deal mainly with the principles underlying rules<br /><br />Beauty is harder to analyze than truth<br /><br />If we must escape from reality, it should be to a deeper or greater reality<br /><br />To discover this reality makes us happy, the experience is deeply satisfying to some part of ourselves we do not ordinarily touch<br /><br />Philosophical books ask the same sort of profound questions that children ask.<br /><br />Do some things exist only in the mind or for the mind, or exist outside the mind, whether or not known to us, or even knowable by us?<br /><br />What is involved in any change?<br /><br />When you learn something you are changed<br /><br />Some remarkable changes as birth and death, local motion, growth, or alteration in quality<br /><br />Are there different types of causes responsible for change?<br /><br />Philosophy today is no longer being written for lay readers<br /><br />Professional philosophers and scientists are not interested in the views of anyone but other experts<br /><br />History is closer to fiction than to science. The historian must always make up something. He assigns causes for events and motivations for actions<br /><br />The causes of every human action were so manifold, so complex, and so deeply hidden in unconscious motivations that it is impossible to know why anything ever happened – <i>Tolstoy</i><br /><br />It is necessary to read more than one account of the history of an event or period if we want to understand it<br /><br />Read a history not only to learn what really happened at a particular time and place in the past, but also to learn the way men act in all times and places, especially now<br /><br />Faith, for those who have it, is the most certain form of knowledge, not a tentative opinion<br /><br />A mind not agitated by good questions cannot appreciate the significance of even the best answers<br /><br />Find the passages in the books that are most germane to your needs<br /><br />Force an author to use your language, rather than using his<br /><br />Tries to look at all sides and to take no sides<br /><br />Don’t cram stuff just so you can say you read X books. Take time to think the information over and it will more easily sink into your brain</span>
  <a data-text-id="11112107143688678099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1329583140" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13418599038746962684">150715: this is a later addition: in answer to the title question 'how to...' I must offer my considered reply, that might be buried, might be forgotten, might be so obvious no one ever states it. how? with joy, with pleasure, with desire, in whatever language, in translations, in genres, in history. to add to this, in personal claim: from a comfortable, shaded, breezy lanai of the condo facing the beach, listening to the surf, the wild chickens, the laughter of children in the pool...<br/><br/>first revi</span>
  <span id="freeText13418599038746962684" style="display:none">150715: this is a later addition: in answer to the title question 'how to...' I must offer my considered reply, that might be buried, might be forgotten, might be so obvious no one ever states it. how? with joy, with pleasure, with desire, in whatever language, in translations, in genres, in history. to add to this, in personal claim: from a comfortable, shaded, breezy lanai of the condo facing the beach, listening to the surf, the wild chickens, the laughter of children in the pool...<br /><br />first review: i read a lot of books. many are not the sort many people read, but nonetheless i believe i read in a widely- if not 'well'- read way. this is of its time 1940, updated 1972. so the five rating is perhaps deceptive. i do not always agree with some of the suggestions, the judgements, even the final implied value of 'how to read'- where this is 'what to read'. i believe it is more important that you read than what you read. i would not presume to tell readers it is best to read 'above their heads', that reading should be done well, even done at all, but this book works with such assertions given to those of us who, well, like to read, value reading, are open to anything that helps this project...<br /><br /> the five stars is sincere, on the other, as so many intro philosophy texts i have read, in that it encourages me to read on, read more, read other books. by way of describing this book, as it suggests, it is useful to simply refer to the organized and complete contents pages: <br /><br />part one: dimensions of reading. 1) activity and art of reading 2) levels of reading 3) elementary reading 4) inspectional reading 5) how to be a demanding reader. <br /><br />part two: third level, analytic reading 6) pigeonholing a book 7) x-raying a book 8) coming to terms with an author 9) determining author's message 10) criticizing a book fairly 11) agreeing or disagreeing with an author 12) aids to reading.<br /><br />part three: approaches to different kinds of reading 13) how to read practical books 14) imaginative literature 15) suggestions for stories, plays, poems 16) history 17) science and math 18) philosophy 19) social science.<br /><br />part four: ultimate goals of reading 20) syntopical reading 21) reading and growth of your mind.<br /><br />for me, i recognize that as the levels progress they blend together and no longer follow each other, that i have part one primarily as unconscious, usually very good, skills. it is good to have them enumerated, examined, so that i might learn consciously. of part two, this is clearest that reading is an active, serious, pragmatic operation, and is familiar awareness, here unstated that it is fruitful to 'stretch your mind'. by part three, it is by order assumed we the readers should learn first the skills of reading nonfiction, that only then can we approach fiction. i have only just read this work, here i am trying to set it in my mind. it is a friendly, helpful, book that offers the reader an awareness if not simply practical advice on how to read. this practical advice is not ignored, but will show up later...<br /><br />i must admit then i am an inconsistent, perhaps mistaken reader. i do not x-ray books, or read blurbs, commentaries, introductions, i hate coming across readers' notations, underlining, highlighting... so never do this myself, i often have the habit of reading nonfiction such as philosophy as if fiction, i do not like to know too much of the author, i often ignore even pointed declarations of commentators and simply go with what is written, i enjoy poetical tendencies in non-poetic work, such as jargon in nonfiction... on the other: yes i often ''syntopically' read many works by the same author, yes i often think too much power is granted to the writer in fiction and we readers should take it back, yes i read several authors on the same arts, read multiple works by this or that philosopher (henri bergson, husserl, heidegger, sartre, merleau-ponty, ) or writer (ross macdonald, robbe-grillet, saramago), i read only ever for fun- but my idea of 'fun' is four hundred pages on 'how to read'...<br /><br />this is not the first book i have read on this subject, simply one of the best to start, then you can go on to, say: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16939.Literary_Theory">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a>, or:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/549369.Literary_Feminisms">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5...</a>, or: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/393978.Critical_Theory_and_Science_Fiction">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3...</a> or: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2124144.Postmodern_Literary_Theory">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2...</a> or: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8462374-the-literature-of-hope-in-the-middle-ages-and-today">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8...</a> or you can go afield: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3948821-russian-pulp">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3...</a> or more technically: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5315572-chinese-theories-of-fiction">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5...</a><br /><br />i enjoyed all of these books, though they are more on poetics, more 'this is what you are reading'...</span>
  <a data-text-id="13418599038746962684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2697179264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2697179264" />
    <a title="Romanas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7309374-romanas"><img alt="Romanas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567834718p2/7309374.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2697179264?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Romanas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Romanas" href="/user/show/7309374-romanas">Romanas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7309374-romanas?shelf=0-re-read">0-re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7309374-romanas?shelf=education">education</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2697179264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18428386379361153142">Having thousands of books behind me, I thought that a book with the title How to Read a Book would fall into the same category as the books about how to walk or how to ride a bicycle. But then there's a question -- how many of those books I really understood and how many of them just gave me information, which would anyway be gone almost completely after a while? This regards primarily to expository books, since reading of literature has a different purpose.<br/><br/>Sometimes, the most obvious is not tha</span>
  <span id="freeText18428386379361153142" style="display:none">Having thousands of books behind me, I thought that a book with the title How to Read a Book would fall into the same category as the books about how to walk or how to ride a bicycle. But then there's a question -- how many of those books I really understood and how many of them just gave me information, which would anyway be gone almost completely after a while? This regards primarily to expository books, since reading of literature has a different purpose.<br /><br />Sometimes, the most obvious is not that obvious, and like in any kind of endeavor there are levels of nerdiness. This one appears to be quite useful. On a global scale, 95 percent of adults are literate and they know how to read. However, the percentage of those who can read well is another story. How many of you, equipped with the normal memorization abilities, can properly recollect what you have read some years ago?<br /><br />For the most part, 99% of the content is gone after a while. I believe though that insights from the books we forget still remain in some corners of our consciousness and they contribute to the wholeness of our thinking. However, in order to boost the capability of thinking and understanding, reading structured and well is of great importance. Mortimer Adler in this old book provides the methodology for doing it. The author suggests four levels of reading: <br /><br />1) Elementary<br />	2) Inspectional<br />	3) Analytical, and <br />	4) Syntopical reading (also known as comparative reading).<br /><br />Many of us read for information, and, usually, that is enough for literature, but when it comes to expository books the aim should be understanding. And there is a big difference between reading for information and reading for understanding.<br /><br />Traditional education seems to be unaware of the levels of reading and seldom provides some structure for reading for proper understanding. Therefore many of us probably have a nagging feeling that we don't know how to read well. The author fills that gap by showing which skills are needed to become good at reading. I believe, If we put hard work and apply the steps proposed the time spent on books will increase in value dramatically.<br /><br />The ideas the authors suggests are worth five stars, but, the writing, unfortunately, is somewhere between two and three. It is a bit tedious and repetitive.<br /><br />I need to admit, I am a very competitive person, and the amount of the books read is one of my personal KPI's - a measure which is quite useless for the reading of non-fiction books. I am going test the suggested style of reading and see if it can make a difference.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18428386379361153142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2747412451" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_179011822" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179011822" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5070927-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307062026p2/5070927.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/179011822?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/5070927-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5070927-paul?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5070927-paul?shelf=studying-thinking">studying-thinking</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5070927-paul?shelf=landmark-books">landmark-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer179011822" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18090341491226401258">It's not how much you read, but how well--and this book gives you specific, step-by-step techniques to get you to read as well as possible.<br/><br/>First of all, who would be so presumptuous as to advise fellow adults on how to read--a skill notionally possessed by everyone who's made it through public school? Well, Mortimer J. Adler, philosopher, longtime editor of the Encyclopedia Britannica, and moving force behind the Britannica Great Books of the Western World series; and Charles Van Doren, Adler's </span>
  <span id="freeText18090341491226401258" style="display:none">It's not how much you read, but how well--and this book gives you specific, step-by-step techniques to get you to read as well as possible.<br /><br />First of all, who would be so presumptuous as to advise fellow adults on how to read--a skill notionally possessed by everyone who's made it through public school? Well, Mortimer J. Adler, philosopher, longtime editor of the Encyclopedia Britannica, and moving force behind the Britannica Great Books of the Western World series; and Charles Van Doren, Adler's colleague at the Institute for Philosophical Research and author of, among other works, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11526306.The_Idea_of_Progress__Concepts_in_Western_Thought_Series_" title="The Idea of Progress (Concepts in Western Thought Series) by Charles Van Doren" rel="nofollow noopener">The Idea of Progress</a>. Observing that most of us don't get very much out of a book when we read it, they set about to help those of us who want to, to get more.<br /><br />Their method applies mostly to reading nonfiction. While they devote 2 chapters out of 21 to reading "imaginative literature", and have useful things to say about such reading, I found these chapters to be the weakest, since imaginative literature lies outside the zone that is most conducive to their analytical approach.<br /><br />But for nonfiction reading they set out a powerful, systematic method for getting the most out of a book.<br /><br />Not all works deserve equally deep reading. Indeed, most books are simply bad according to the authors, and not worth reading at all. Many others might contain information or ideas that are useful, without being especially significant overall. A few--a very few--are the considered, mature works by the best minds in the history of recorded thought. These, the authors assert, merit our close attention and careful reading and rereading.<br /><br />So the authors introduce us to the "levels of reading", of which they set out four:<br /><br />1. Elementary reading: This is the basic skill of being able to read with comprehension. Do we have it? If not, we need to work on this.<br /><br />2. Inspectional reading: This is how to assess a book's promise quickly by examining its table of contents, index, and flap blurbs, and by skimming key parts of the text.<br /><br />3. Analytical reading: This section of 6 chapters forms the core of the book. Here you learn how to look for the key arguments of a book, summarize these, and discover any special meanings the book's author is giving certain terms, as well as how to criticize the book fairly and determine where you stand in relation to the author's main thesis.<br /><br />4. Syntopical reading: This is the term the authors give to the highest, most demanding level of reading--the level required for serious research that requires the comparison of different texts. Syntopical reading is possible only for those who already have a decent grasp of analytical reading.<br /><br />The authors admit that to follow their method completely is hard, time-consuming work, and appropriate only for the most worthy texts. That's why it's important to master the skills of inspectional reading, which allow us to gain a quick idea of which books deserve this deluxe treatment.<br /><br />I myself have applied Adler and Van Doren's methods to only a few books so far, and have not completed the method for any one book (it is indeed a lot of work). The one I've gone furthest with so far is an analytical reading of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1758788.A_Free_Nation_Deep_in_Debt_The_Financial_Roots_of_Democracy" title="A Free Nation Deep in Debt The Financial Roots of Democracy by James MacDonald" rel="nofollow noopener">A Free Nation Deep in Debt: The Financial Roots of Democracy</a> by James Macdonald, a book that, in my view, amply deserves such a reading. Even though I have not got to the end of my analytical reading, by following Adler and Van Doren's methods I have very much deepened and clarified my understanding of Macdonald's book, and I know that if I finish my analysis I will be able to say I have truly read it.<br /><br />I'm currently reading the Organon of Aristotle, and I intend to apply the Adler-Van Doren method to these books when I'm done. I've already made a start with the <i>Categories</i>, and it's already bearing fruit in the form of increased comprehension.<br /><br />If you're serious about reading, you owe it to yourself to read this book; and the more serious you are, the more important it is that you make an appointment with this book. Read it well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18090341491226401258" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
